especialy with the cheese... alot of cheese...cheese being a nutritious food consisting primarily of the curd, the semisolid substance formed when milk curdles, or coagulates. Curdling occurs naturally if milk is not used promptly: it sours, forming an acid curd, which releases whey, a watery fluid containing the soluble constituents; and it leaves semisolid curd, or fresh cheese.

The Origins Of Cheese
The history of cheese dates back thousands of years. While the exact origin of cheese is uncertain, it is believed to have been discovered independently by different ancient civilizations. The process of cheese-making likely began when early humans stored milk in containers made from animal stomachs, inadvertently causing the milk to curdle and separate into curds and whey. This accidental curdling eventually led to the development of cheese-making techniques.
Ancient Cheese-Making The art of cheese-making spread across ancient civilizations, including the Egyptians, Greeks, and Romans. These cultures developed various methods to produce and preserve cheese. In Egypt, archaeological evidence suggests that cheese was made as early as 2,000 BCE. The Greeks and Romans, known for their culinary prowess, not only consumed cheese but also attributed it with medicinal properties.
Ancient Cheese-Making Techniques As cheese-making techniques spread across different civilizations, various cultures developed their own methods and preferences for cheese production. The Romans, for instance, were known for their love of soft, fresh cheeses like ricotta and mozzarella. They also introduced the concept of aging cheese, which allowed for the development of more complex flavors and textures.
The Role Of Cheese In Ancient Civilizations In addition to being a source of sustenance, cheese held cultural and religious significance in many ancient civilizations. In ancient Egypt, cheese was offered as a sacred food to the gods, and it was often included in burial rituals. The Greeks believed that cheese was a gift from the gods and associated it with divinity and abundance.

Cheese In The Middle Ages During the Middle Ages, cheese-making techniques continued to evolve. Monasteries played a significant role in the development and preservation of cheese-making knowledge. Monks, who were often skilled in agriculture and husbandry, experimented with different methods and techniques, leading to the creation of new cheese varieties. These cheeses became a valuable source of nutrition and a means of trade for monastic communities.
European Cheese-Making Traditions Europe has a long and storied history of cheese-making. Different regions developed distinct cheese-making traditions, resulting in a wide array of cheeses with unique flavors and textures. From the pungent Roquefort of France to the creamy Gouda of the Netherlands, each cheese tells a story of its origin and the cultural heritage of its creators.
Cheese In The New World When European explorers and settlers ventured to the New World, they brought their cheese-making traditions with them. Cheese production began in the 17th century in North America, and it quickly became a staple in the diets of early American colonists. Cheddar, in particular, gained popularity and became one of the most widely produced and consumed cheeses in the United States.
The Rise Of Artisanal Cheese While industrialization brought convenience and accessibility, it also led to a loss of traditional cheese-making practices and the homogenization of flavors. In response, a resurgence of artisanal cheese-making began in the latter half of the 20th century. Artisan cheese-makers focused on preserving traditional techniques, using high-quality ingredients, and creating unique and flavorful cheeses. This movement emphasized the importance of terroir, the influence of local environment and methods on the taste and character of cheese.
The Industrial Revolution And Cheese Production The Industrial Revolution brought significant changes to cheese production. With advancements in technology and transportation, cheese production shifted from small-scale, artisanal methods to larger-scale industrial production. This led to increased efficiency and accessibility of cheese, making it more widely available to people around the world. With the advent of mechanization and industrial processes, cheese-making became more efficient and standardized. This led to the creation of cheese factories and the mass production of cheeses like Cheddar and Swiss. Advances in refrigeration and transportation also allowed for the distribution of cheese over long distances.
Modern Cheese-Making Today, cheese-making has reached new heights of innovation and creativity. Artisanal cheese-makers continue to honor traditional techniques while adopting modern technologies to refine their craft. The demand for high-quality, locally sourced cheeses has grown, leading to the resurgence of small-scale, artisan cheese producers who prioritize craftsmanship and unique flavor profiles. Today, the world of cheese continues to evolve. Cheesemakers experiment with new flavors, textures, and aging techniques, pushing the boundaries of what cheese can be. From infused cheeses with herbs and spices to cheeses aged in unique environments like caves or wine barrels, there is no shortage of creativity in the cheese-making industry.
Types Of Cheese And Their Unique Characteristics Fresh Cheeses Fresh cheeses are typically unaged and have a high moisture content. They are soft, creamy, and spreadable in texture. Some popular examples of fresh cheeses include:
Ricotta: Ricotta is a creamy Italian cheese made from the whey leftover from cheese production. It has a mild flavor and is commonly used in both sweet and savory dishes.
Mozzarella: Mozzarella is a classic Italian cheese known for its stretchy texture and mild, milky flavor. It is commonly used in pizzas, caprese salads, and other Italian dishes.
Cottage Cheese: Cottage cheese is a mild and creamy cheese made from the curds of cow’s milk. It has a slightly lumpy texture and is often enjoyed as a healthy snack or used in recipes like salads and desserts
Soft-Ripened Cheeses Soft-ripened cheeses have a bloomy rind and a creamy interior. They are characterized by their rich, buttery flavors and smooth textures. Examples of soft-ripened cheeses include:
Brie: It is a popular French cheese with a velvety texture and a rich, earthy flavor. It has a white, edible rind and pairs well with fruits, crackers, and crusty bread.
Camembert: It is another French cheese similar to Brie. It has a creamy, gooey texture and a slightly stronger flavor. It is often enjoyed with bread or used in baked dishes.
Chaource: It is a creamy French cheese made from cow’s milk. It has a soft, bloomy rind and a delicate, tangy flavor. It is commonly used in salads, cheese boards, and desserts
Semi-Soft Cheeses Semi-soft cheeses have a smooth and pliable texture. They are often mild in flavor and can range from slightly tangy to nutty. Some examples of semi-soft cheeses include:
Havarti: It is a Danish cheese known for its buttery and creamy texture. It has small, irregular holes and a mild, slightly tangy flavor. It melts well, making it a popular choice for sandwiches and grilled cheese.
Gouda: It is a Dutch cheese with a smooth and supple texture. It has a slightly sweet and nutty flavor, and it can range from mild to aged with more pronounced flavors. It is often enjoyed on cheese platters or used in cooking.
Fontina: It is an Italian cheese with a semi-soft texture and a rich, nutty flavor. It melts beautifully, making it an excellent choice for fondues, gratins, and sandwiches.
Hard Cheeses Hard cheeses are aged for a longer period, resulting in a firm and crumbly texture. They often have complex flavors that intensify with age. Some well-known hard cheeses include:
Cheddar: It is a widely popular cheese with a crumbly texture and a sharp, tangy flavor. It comes in various ages, ranging from mild to extra sharp. Cheddar is versatile and can be used in sandwiches, macaroni and cheese, or enjoyed on its own. Parmesan: Parmesan, also known as Parmigiano-Reggiano, is an Italian cheese with a hard, granular texture. It has a nutty and savory flavor and is commonly grated over pasta, salads, and soups.
Gruyère: Gruyère is a Swiss cheese with a firm and dense texture. It has a rich, nutty flavor with hints of sweetness. Gruyère is often used in fondues, quiches, and gratins.
Thes> Cheese As A Cultural Icon Cheese has become not only a culinary delight but also a symbol of cultural identity. In many countries, specific cheeses are deeply intertwined with their cultural heritage. For example, in France, cheese is considered an essential part of the gastronomic tradition, and the country boasts over 400 distinct types of cheese. br> Cheese Around The World Cheese has become a global phenomenon, with different countries boasting their own iconic cheeses. From the tangy Cheddar of England to the sharp Parmigiano-Reggiano of Italy, each cheese carries its own cultural significance and regional pride. Exploring the world of cheese allows us to experience the rich diversity of flavors and textures that have been crafted over centuries.

The Future Of Cheese As the global food landscape continues to change, so does the cheese industry. With an increasing focus on sustainability and ethical practices, there is a growing interest in locally sourced and artisanal cheeses. Additionally, advancements in technology and scientific research are opening up new possibilities for cheese production, such as plant-based alternatives and precision fermentation.
The history of cheese is a testament to human ingenuity and culinary exploration. From its humble beginnings as an accidental discovery to the vast array of cheeses enjoyed today, cheese has evolved alongside human civilization. Whether you savor a slice of Brie, crumble some Feta on your salad, or indulge in a creamy Camembert, each bite offers a taste of history and a connection to the past. So, the next time you enjoy a delicious piece of cheese, take a moment to appreciate the centuries of knowledge and craftsmanship that have gone into creating this timeless delight.

and about pizza ?...well...Pizza is the world’s favourite fast food. We eat it everywhere – at home, in restaurants, on street corners. Some three billion pizzas are sold each year in the United States alone, an average of 46 slices per person. But the story of how the humble pizza came to enjoy such global dominance reveals much about the history of migration, economics and technological change.


People have been eating pizza, in one form or another, for centuries. As far back as antiquity, pieces of flatbread, topped with savouries, served as a simple and tasty meal for those who could not afford plates, or who were on the go. These early pizzas appear in Virgil’s Aeneid. Shortly after arriving in Latium, Aeneas and his crew sat down beneath a tree and laid out ‘thin wheaten cakes as platters for their meal’. They then scattered them with mushrooms and herbs they had found in the woods and guzzled them down, crust and all, prompting Aeneas’ son Ascanius to exclaim: “Look! We’ve even eaten our plates!”


Pizza for breakfast : But it was in late 18th-century Naples that the pizza as we now know it came into being. Under the Bourbon kings, Naples had become one of the largest cities in Europe – and it was growing fast. Fuelled by overseas trade and a steady influx of peasants from the countryside, its population ballooned from 200,000 in 1700 to 399,000 in 1748. As the urban economy struggled to keep pace, an ever greater number of the city’s inhabitants fell into poverty. The most abject of these were known as lazzaroni, because their ragged appearance resembled that of Lazarus. Numbering around 50,000 they scraped by on the pittance they earned as porters, messengers or casual labourers. Always rushing about in search of work, they needed food that was cheap and easy to eat. Pizzas met this need. Sold not in shops, but by street vendors carrying huge boxes under their arms, they would be cut to meet the customer’s budget or appetite. As Alexandre Dumas noted in Le Corricolo (1843), a two liard slice would make a good breakfast, while two sous would buy a pizza large enough for a whole family. None of them were terribly complicated. Though similar in some respects to Virgil’s flatbreads, they were now defined by inexpensive, easy-to-find ingredients with plenty of flavour. The simplest were topped with nothing more than garlic, lard and salt. But others included caciocavallo (a cheese made from horse’s milk), cecenielli (whitebait) or basil. Some even had tomatoes on top. Only recently introduced from the Americas, these were still a curiosity, looked down upon by contemporary gourmets. But it was their unpopularity – and hence their low price – that made them attractive.
For a long time, pizzas were scorned by food writers. Associated with the crushing poverty of the lazzaroni, they were frequently denigrated as ‘disgusting’, especially by foreign visitors. In 1831, Samuel Morse – inventor of the telegraph – described pizza as a ‘species of the most nauseating cake … covered over with slices of pomodoro or tomatoes, and sprinkled with little fish and black pepper and I know not what other ingredients, it altogether looks like a piece of bread that has been taken reeking out of the sewer’.
When the first cookbooks appeared in the late 19th century, they pointedly ignored pizza. Even those dedicated to Neapolitan cuisine disdained to mention it – despite the fact that the gradual improvement in the lazzaroni’s status had prompted the appearance of the first pizza restaurants.


Royal approval : All that changed after Italian unification. While on a visit to Naples in 1889, King Umberto I and Queen Margherita grew tired of the complicated French dishes they were served for breakfast, lunch and dinner. Hastily summoned to prepare some local specialities for the queen, the pizzaiolo Raffaele Esposito cooked three sorts of pizza: one with lard, caciocavallo and basil; another with cecenielli; and a third with tomatoes, mozzarella and basil. The queen was delighted. Her favourite – the last of the three – was christened pizza margherita in her honour.
This signalled an important shift. Margherita’s seal of approval not only elevated the pizza from being a food fit only for lazzaroni to being something a royal family could enjoy, but also transformed pizza from a local into a truly national dish. It introduced the notion that pizza was a genuinely Italian food – akin to pasta and polenta.
Nevertheless, pizza was slow to move out of Naples. The initial spur was provided by migration. From the 1930s onwards, a growing number of Neapolitans moved northwards in search of work, taking their cuisine with them. This trend was accelerated by war. When Allied soldiers invaded Italy in 1943-4, they were so taken with the pizza they encountered in Campania that they asked for it wherever else they went. But it was tourism – facilitated by the declining cost of travel in the postwar period – that really consolidated pizza’s position as a truly Italian dish. As tourists became increasingly curious about Italian food, restaurants throughout the peninsula started offering more regional specialities – including pizza. The quality was, at first, variable – not every restaurant had a pizza oven. Nevertheless, pizza quickly spread throughout Italy. As it did so, new ingredients were introduced in response to local tastes and the higher prices that customers were now willing to pay.


Pizza goes west : But it was in America that pizza found its second home. By the end of the 19th century, Italian emigrants had already reached the East Coast; and in 1905, the first pizzeria – Lombardi’s – was opened in New York City. Soon, pizza became an American institution. Spreading across the country in step with the growing pace of urbanisation, it was quickly taken up by enterprising restaurateurs (who were often not from an Italian background) and adapted to reflect local tastes, identities and needs. Shortly after the US entered the Second World War, a Texan named Ike Sewell attempted to attract new customers to his newly opened Chicago pizzeria by offering a much ‘heartier’ version of the dish, complete with a deeper, thicker crust and richer, more abundant toppings – usually with cheese at the bottom and a mountain of chunky tomato sauce heaped on top of it. At about the same time, the Rocky Mountain Pie was developed in Colorado. Although not as deep as its Chicago relative, it had a much wider crust, which was meant to be eaten with honey as a desert. In time, these were even joined by a Hawaiian version, topped with ham and pineapple – much to the bewilderment of Neapolitans.
From the 1950s onwards, the rapid pace of economic and technological change in the US transformed the pizza even more radically. Two changes are worthy of note. The first was the ‘domestication’ of pizza. As disposable incomes grew, fridges and freezers became increasingly common and demand for ‘convenience’ foods grew – prompting the development of the frozen pizza. Designed to be taken home and cooked at will, this required changes to be made to the recipe. Instead of being scattered with generous slices of tomato, the base was now smothered with a smooth tomato paste, which served to prevent the dough from drying out during oven cooking; and new cheeses had to be developed to withstand freezing. The second change was the ‘commercialisation’ of pizza. With the growing availability of cars and motorcycles, it became possible to deliver freshly cooked food to customers’ doors – and pizza was among the first dishes to be served up. In 1960, Tom and James Monaghan founded ‘Dominik’s’ in Michigan and, after winning a reputation for speedy delivery, took their company – which they renamed ‘Domino’s’ – nationwide. They and their competitors expanded abroad, so that now there is scarcely a city in the world where they cannot be found.
Paradoxically, the effect of these changes was to make pizza both more standardised and more susceptible to variation. While the form – a dough base, topped with thin layers of tomato and cheese – became more firmly entrenched, the need to appeal to customers’ desire for novelty led to ever more elaborate varieties being offered, so that now Pizza Hut in Poland sells a spicy ‘Indian’ version and Domino’s in Japan has developed an ‘Elvis’ pizza, with just about everything on it.
Today’s pizzas are far removed from those of the lazzaroni; and many pizza purists – especially in Naples – balk at some of the more outlandish toppings that are now on offer. But pizza is still recognisable as pizza and centuries of social, economic and technological change are baked into every slice.
A recipe for pizza marinara
Alexander Lee is a fellow in the Centre for the Study of the Renaissance at the University of Warwick.
